0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;">\n<b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Replacement_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For a guide to IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Manx</b> (native name <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Gaelg</i> or <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Gailck</i>, pronounced <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Manx" title="Help:IPA/Manx">[\xc9\xa1ilg]</a></span> or <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Manx" title="Help:IPA/Manx">[\xc9\xa1ilk]</a></span> or <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Manx" title="Help:IPA/Manx">[\xc9\xa1e\xcb\x90lg]</a></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> also known as <b>Manx Gaelic</b>, and also historically spelled <b>Manks</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic Celtic language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European language">Indo-European language</a> family, that was spoken as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Manx_people" title="Manx people">Manx people</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> until the death of the last <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">native speaker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ned_Maddrell" title="Ned Maddrell">Ned Maddrell</a>, in 1974. Despite this, the language has never fallen completely out of use, with a minority having some knowledge of it and Manx still has a role as an important part of the island\'s culture and heritage. Manx has been the subject of <a href="/wiki/Language_revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Language revival">language revival</a> efforts with estimates, in 2015, of around 1,800 people with varying levels of <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> conversational ability. Since the late 20th century, Manx has become more visible on the island, with increased signage, radio broadcasts and a bilingual primary school. The revival of Manx has been made easier because the language was well-recorded; for example, the Bible had been translated into Manx, and audio recordings had been made of native speakers.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Numbers"><span class="tocnumber">12.8</span> <span class="toctext">Numbers</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Syntax"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Syntax</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Vocabulary"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Vocabulary</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Loanwords"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Loanwords</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#Comparative_vocabulary_examples"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Comparative vocabulary examples</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_of_the_language">Names of the language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names of the language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Manx">In Manx</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: In Manx">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In Manx, the language is called <i>Gaelg</i> or <i>Gailck</i> (pronounced "gilk" or "gilg" or "gelg" with hard Gs), a word which shares the same etymology as the word "Gaelic", borrowed from Northern Irish. The sister languages of <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> use <i>Gaeilge</i> (dialect variants Gaoluinn, Gaedhlag, Gaelge and Gaelic) and <i>G\xc3\xa0idhlig</i>, respectively, for their languages. As with Irish and Scottish, the form with the <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite article</a> is frequently used in Manx, e.g. <i>y Ghaelg</i> or <i>y Ghailck</i> (Irish <i>an Ghaeilge</i>, Scottish <i>a\' Gh\xc3\xa0idhlig</i>).\n</p><p>To distinguish it from the two other forms of Gaelic, the phrases <i>Gaelg</i>/<i>Gailck Vannin</i> (Gaelic of <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Mann</a>) and <i>Gaelg</i>/<i>Gailck Vanninnagh</i> (Manx Gaelic) also are used. In addition, the nickname "\xc3\x87hengey ny Mayrey" (the mother tongue/tongue of the mother, lit. the mother\'s tongue) is occasionally used.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_English">In English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: In English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The language is usually referred to in English as "Manx". The term "Manx Gaelic" is often used, for example when discussing the relationship between the three Goidelic languages (Irish, Scottish Gaelic, and Manx) or to avoid confusion with <a href="/wiki/Manx_English" title="Manx English">Anglo-Manx</a>, the form of English spoken on the island. Scottish Gaelic is often referred to in English as simply "Gaelic", but this is less common with Manx and Irish.\n</p><p>A feature of Anglo-Manx deriving from Gaelic is the use of the definite article, e.g. "the Manx", "the Gaelic", in ways not generally seen in standard English.\n</p><p>The word "Manx" is frequently spelled "Manks" in historical sources, particularly those written by natives of the island; the word means "Mannish", and originates from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>Mannisk</i>. The name of the island, Man, is frequently spelled "Mann". It is sometimes accompanied by a footnote explaining that it is a two-syllable word, with the stress on the first syllable, "MAN-en". The island is named after the Irish god <a href="/wiki/Manann%C3%A1n_mac_Lir" title="Manann\xc3\xa1n mac Lir">Manann\xc3\xa1n mac Lir</a>, thus <i>Ellan Vannin</i> (Irish <i>Oile\xc3\xa1n Mhannan\xc3\xa1in</i>) \'Mannan\xc3\xa1n\'s Island\'.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clagh_Ogham_ec_Thie_Tashtee_Vannin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Clagh_Ogham_ec_Thie_Tashtee_Vannin.jpg/200px-Clagh_Ogham_ec_Thie_Tashtee_Vannin.jpg" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Clagh_Ogham_ec_Thie_Tashtee_Vannin.jpg/300px-Clagh_Ogham_ec_Thie_Tashtee_Vannin.jpg 1.5x, class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clagh_Ogham_ec_Thie_Tashtee_Vannin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Ogham_inscription" title="Ogham inscription">ogham inscription</a> on a stone in the <a href="/wiki/Manx_Museum" title="Manx Museum">Manx Museum</a> written in Primitive Irish and which reads DOVAIDONA MAQI DROATA, "Of Dovaido, son of Droata"<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Illiam_Dhone.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Illiam_Dhone.JPG/200px-Illiam_Dhone.JPG" width="200" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Illiam_Dhone.JPG/300px-Illiam_Dhone.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Illiam_Dhone.JPG/400px-Illiam_Dhone.JPG 2x" data-file-width="465" data-file-height="745" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Illiam_Dhone.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>William Christian, better known as <a href="/wiki/Illiam_Dhone" title="Illiam Dhone">Illiam Dhone</a> (Brown-haired William)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lag_ny_Keeilley,_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Lag_ny_Keeilley%2C_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg/200px-Lag_ny_Keeilley%2C_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Lag_ny_Keeilley%2C_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg/300px-Lag_ny_Keeilley%2C_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Lag_ny_Keeilley%2C_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg/400px-Lag_ny_Keeilley%2C_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lag_ny_Keeilley,_West_slope_of_Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_181312.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Lag ny Keeilley</i> (<i>Hollow of the Church</i>) on <a href="/wiki/Cronk_ny_Arrey_Laa" title="Cronk ny Arrey Laa">Cronk ny Arrey Laa</a> (Hill of the Day Watch). The Manx language has had a substantial influence on the island\'s <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">toponymy</a> and nomenclature.</div></div></div>\n<p>Manx is a <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic</a> language, closely related to <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>. On the whole it is partially mutually intelligible with these, and native speakers of one find it easy to gain passive, and even spoken, competency in the other two.\n</p><p>The earliest known language of the Isle of Man was a form of <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic</a> (the language which developed into Welsh, Cornish and Breton); however, like <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> and modern Irish, Manx is descended from <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Irish" title="Primitive Irish">Primitive Irish</a>, which is first attested in <a href="/wiki/Ogham" title="Ogham">Ogham</a> inscriptions from the 4th century AD. These writings have been found throughout Ireland and the west coast of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>. Primitive Irish transitioned into <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> through the 5th century. Old Irish, dating from the 6th century, used the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> and is attested primarily in <a href="/wiki/Marginalia" title="Marginalia">marginalia</a> to Latin manuscripts, but there are no extant examples from the Isle of Man. By the 10th century Old Irish had evolved into <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a>, which was spoken throughout Ireland, in Scotland and in the Isle of Man. Like the coastal areas of Scotland and Ireland, the Isle of Man was colonised by the Norse, who left their legacy in certain loanwords, personal names, and place names such as <a href="/wiki/Laxey" title="Laxey">Laxey</a> (Laksaa) and <a href="/wiki/Ramsey,_Isle_of_Man" title="Ramsey, Isle of Man">Ramsey</a> (Rhumsaa). However, they made very little impact on the language overall.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the later Middle Ages, the Isle of Man fell increasingly under the influence of England, and from then on the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> has been the chief external factor in the development of Manx. Beginning in 1405, Manx experienced even more English influence under the rule of Sir John Stanley. As contact between Manx speakers and Gaelic speakers from Scotland and Ireland declined, the language diverged further from its related neighbours.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 17th century, some university students left the Isle of Man to attend school in England. At the same time, teaching in English was required in schools founded by governor Isaac Barrow. Barrow also promoted the use of English in churches; he considered that it was a superior language for reading the Bible; however, because the majority of ministers were monolingual Manx speakers, his views had little practical impact.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas Wilson began his tenure as Bishop of Mann in 1698 and was succeeded by Mark Hildesley. Both men held positive views of Manx; Wilson was the first person to publish a book in Manx, a translation of <i>The Principles and Duties of Christianity</i> (<i>Coyrie Sodjey</i>), and Hildesley successfully promoted the use of Manx as the language of instruction in schools. The New Testament was first published in Manx in 1767.  In the late 18th century, nearly every school was teaching in English. This decline continued into the 19th century, as English gradually became the primary language spoken on the Isle of Man.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1848, J.&#160;G. Cumming wrote, "there are ... few persons (perhaps none of the young) who speak no English." <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jenner" title="Henry Jenner">Henry Jenner</a> estimated in 1874 that about 30% of the population habitually spoke Manx (12,340 out of a population of 41,084). According to official census figures, 9.1% of the population claimed to speak Manx in 1901; in 1921 the percentage was only 1.1%.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Since the language was used by so few people, it had low linguistic "<a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">prestige</a>", and parents tended to not teach Manx to their children, thinking it would be useless to them compared with English.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revival">Revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Following the decline in the use of Manx during the 19th century, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text"><a href="/wiki/Manx_Gaelic_Society" title="Manx Gaelic Society">Yn \xc3\x87heshaght Ghailckagh</a></i> (The Manx Language Society) was founded in 1899. By the middle of the 20th century, only a few elderly <a href="/wiki/Native_speaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Native speaker">native speakers</a> remained (the last of them, <a href="/wiki/Ned_Maddrell" title="Ned Maddrell">Ned Maddrell</a>, died on December 27, 1974), but by then a scholarly revival had begun and a few individuals had started teaching it in schools. The Manx Language Unit was formed in 1992, consisting of three members and headed by Manx Language Officer <a href="/wiki/Brian_Stowell" title="Brian Stowell">Brian Stowell</a>, "which was put in charge of all aspects of Manx language teaching and accreditation in schools."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> This led to an increased interest in studying the Manx language and encouraged a renewed sense of ethnic identity. The revival of Manx has been aided by the recording work done in the 20th century by researchers. Most notably, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Folklore_Commission" title="Irish Folklore Commission">Irish Folklore Commission</a> was sent in with recording equipment in 1948 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89amon_de_Valera" title="\xc3\x89amon de Valera">\xc3\x89amon de Valera</a>. Also important in preserving the Manx language was work conducted by language enthusiast and fluent speaker <a href="/wiki/Brian_Stowell" title="Brian Stowell">Brian Stowell</a>, who is considered personally responsible for the current revival of the Manx language.<sup id="cite_ref-graun_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graun-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The Manx Language Strategy was released in 2017, outlining a five-year plan for the language\'s continued revitalization.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2009, <a href="/wiki/Unesco" class="mw-redirect" title="Unesco">Unesco</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Atlas_of_the_World%27s_Languages_in_Danger" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlas of the World&#39;s Languages in Danger">Atlas of the World\'s Languages in Danger</a> declared Manx as an <a href="/wiki/Extinct_language" title="Extinct language">extinct language</a>, despite the presence of hundreds of speakers on the Isle of Man.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Since then, Unesco\'s classification of the language has changed to "critically endangered".<sup id="cite_ref-graun_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graun-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2011 census, 1,823 out of 80,398 Isle of Man residents, or 2.27% of the population, claimed to have knowledge of Manx,<sup id="cite_ref-Gov.im_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gov.im-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> an increase of 134 people from the 2001 census.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> These were spread roughly uniformly over the island: in <a href="/wiki/Douglas,_Isle_of_Man" title="Douglas, Isle of Man">Douglas</a> 566 people professed an ability to speak, read or write Manx; 179 in <a href="/wiki/Peel,_Isle_of_Man" title="Peel, Isle of Man">Peel</a>, 146 in <a href="/wiki/Onchan" title="Onchan">Onchan</a>, and 149 in Ramsey.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Traditional Manx given names are once again becoming common on the Isle of Man, especially <i>Moirrey</i> and <i>Voirrey</i> (Mary, properly pronounced similarly to the Scottish <i>Moira</i>, but often mispronounced as <i>Moiree/Voiree</i> when used as a given name by non-Manx speakers), <i>Illiam</i> (<a href="/wiki/William_(name)" class="mw-redirect" title="William (name)">William</a>), <i>Orry</i> (from the Manx King of Norse origin), <i>Breeshey</i> (also <i>Breesha</i>) (<a href="/wiki/Bridget_(given_name)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridget (given name)">Bridget</a>), <i>Aalish</i> (also <i>Ealish</i>) (<a href="/wiki/Alice_(given_name)" title="Alice (given name)">Alice</a>), <i>Juan</i> (<a href="/wiki/Jack_(name)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack (name)">Jack</a>), <i>Ean</i> (Ian), <i>Joney</i> (<a href="/wiki/John_(name)" class="mw-redirect" title="John (name)">John</a>), <i>Fenella</i> (<a href="/wiki/Fionnuala" title="Fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>), <i>Pherick</i> (<a href="/wiki/Patrick_(given_name)" title="Patrick (given name)">Patrick</a>) and <i>Freya</i> (from the <a href="/wiki/Freyja" title="Freyja">Norse Goddess</a>) remain popular.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Those who have learned Manx as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> as part of the Manx language revival speak what has been called "Neo-Manx".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In Neo-Manx the grammar is different to the Manx spoken to by native speakers like Ned Maddrell and English words have been resubstituted by Gaelic words, leading some to say the new speakers of the language are not speaking "true Manx".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline"
