href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>1.95%\n</td>\n<td>78,266\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2011\n</td>\n<td>1,650<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>1.97%\n</td>\n<td>84,497\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2015\n</td>\n<td>1,800<sup id="cite_ref-graun_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graun-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>2%\n</td>\n<td>88,000\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Manx_literature" title="Manx literature">Manx literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_literature" title="Gaelic literature">Gaelic literature</a></div>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small><i>(November 2010)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Because Manx has never had a large number of speakers, it has never been practical to produce large amounts of written literature. However, a body of oral literature did exist. The "<a href="/wiki/Fianna" title="Fianna">Fianna</a>" tales and others like them are known, including the Manx ballad <i>Fin as Oshin</i>, commemorating <a href="/wiki/Finn_MacCool" class="mw-redirect" title="Finn MacCool">Finn MacCool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ossian" title="Ossian">Ossian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> With the coming of Protestantism, Manx spoken tales slowly disappeared, while a tradition of <i>carvals</i> - religious songs or carols - developed with religious sanction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2009)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>As far as is known, there was no distinctively Manx written literature before the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>. By that time, any presumed literary link with Ireland and Scotland, such as through Irish-trained priests, had been lost. The first published literature in Manx was <i>The Principles and Duties of Christianity (Coyrie Sodjey)</i>, translated by Bishop of Man Thomas Wilson.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> was translated by <a href="/wiki/John_Philips_(bishop)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Philips (bishop)">John Phillips</a>, the Welsh-born <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Sodor_and_Man" title="Bishop of Sodor and Man">Bishop of Sodor and Man</a> (1605\xe2\x80\x9333). The early Manx script has some similarities with orthographical systems found occasionally in Scotland and in Ireland for the transliteration of Gaelic, such as the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Dean_of_Lismore" title="Book of the Dean of Lismore">Book of the Dean of Lismore</a>, as well as some extensive texts based on English and Scottish English orthographical practices of the time. Little secular <a href="/wiki/Manx_literature" title="Manx literature">Manx literature</a> has been preserved.\n</p><p>The New Testament was first published in 1767. When the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican church">Anglican church</a> authorities started to produce written literature in the Manx language in the 18th century, the system developed by John Philips was further "anglicised"; the one feature retained from Welsh orthography was the use of &#x27e8;y&#x27e9; to represent <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">schwa</a> (e.g. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">cabbyl</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ka\xcb\x90\xce\xb2\xc9\x99l]</span> "horse" and <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">cooney</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ku\xcb\x90n\xc9\x99]</span> "help" as well as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\xaa/</span> (e.g. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">fys</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[f\xc9\xaaz]</span> "knowledge"), though it is also used to represent <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[j]</span>, as in English (e.g. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">y Yuan</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99 ju\xcb\x90an]</span> "John" (vocative), <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">yeeast</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ji\xcb\x90\xc9\x99st]</span> "fish").\n</p><p>Other works produced in the 18th and 19th century include catechisms, hymn books and religious tracts. A translation of <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i> was made in 1796.\n</p><p>A considerable amount of secular literature has been produced in the 20th and 21st centuries as part of the language revival. In 2006, the first full-length novel in Manx, <i>Dunveryssyn yn Tooder-Folley</i> (<i>The Vampire Murders</i>) was published by Brian Stowell, after being serialised in the press. There is an increasing amount of literature available in the language, and recent publications include Manx versions of the <i>Gruffalo</i> and <i>Gruffalo\'s Child</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_recognition">Official recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Official recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Manx is not officially recognised by any national or regional government, although its contribution to Manx culture and tradition is acknowledged by some governmental and non-governmental bodies. For example: \n</p><p>The Standing Orders of the House of Keys provide that: "The proceedings of the House shall be in English; but if a Member at any point pronounces a customary term or sentence in Manx Gaelic or any other language, the Speaker may call upon the Member for a translation."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Manx is used in the annual <a href="/wiki/Tynwald" title="Tynwald">Tynwald</a> ceremony and Manx words are used in official <a href="/wiki/Tynwald" title="Tynwald">Tynwald</a> publications.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the purpose of strengthening its contribution to local culture and community, Manx is recognised under the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a> and in the framework of the <a href="/wiki/British-Irish_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="British-Irish Council">British-Irish Council</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BunscoillSignageOct2017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/BunscoillSignageOct2017.jpg/200px-BunscoillSignageOct2017.jpg" width="200" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/BunscoillSignageOct2017.jpg/300px-BunscoillSignageOct2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/BunscoillSignageOct2017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="186" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BunscoillSignageOct2017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sign at the Bunscoill Ghaelgagh at St John\'s</div></div></div>\n<p>Manx is taught as a second language at all of the island\'s primary and secondary schools. The lessons are optional and instruction is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man_Department_of_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Man Department of Education">Department of Education</a>\'s Manx Language Team which teach up to <a href="/wiki/A_Level" class="mw-redirect" title="A Level">A Level</a> standard.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bunscoill_Ghaelgagh" title="Bunscoill Ghaelgagh">Bunscoill Ghaelgagh</a>, a primary school at <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s,_Isle_of_Man" title="St John&#39;s, Isle of Man">St John\'s</a>, has 67 children, as of September 2016, who receive nearly all of their education through the medium of the language. Children who have attended the school have the opportunity to receive some of their secondary education through the language at Queen Elizabeth II High School in <a href="/wiki/Peel,_Isle_of_Man" title="Peel, Isle of Man">Peel</a>.\n</p><p>The playgroup organisation <a href="/wiki/Mooinjer_Veggey" class="mw-redirect" title="Mooinjer Veggey">Mooinjer Veggey</a>, which operates the <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Bunscoill Ghaelgagh</i>, runs a series of preschool groups that introduce the language.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Learning_the_language">Learning the language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Learning the language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are an increasing number of resources available for those wanting to learn the language. The Manx Language Development Officer for <a href="/wiki/Culture_Vannin" title="Culture Vannin">Culture Vannin</a> manages the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.learnmanx.com/">Learnmanx.com</a> website which has a wide variety of resources. These include <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.culturevannin.im/cms/page_288619.html">mobile apps</a> a <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://greinneyder.podomatic.com/">new podcast</a> in Manx, the 1000 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.learnmanx.com/cms/1000words.html">words-in-Manx</a> challenge and the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list=PLY5y-gRhKs8jPbIKdBEogUP_TXuqcPQfN">Video-a-day</a> in Manx series.\nThe most recent development on the adult language front is the creation of a new on-line course, Say Something in Manx <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://www.saysomethingin.com/manx">[1]</a> which has been created in conjunction with the Say Something in Welsh <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://www.saysomethingin.com/welsh">[2]</a> \nIt is hoped that this will be the main way on-line learners will access the language from now on.\n2016 also saw the launch of a new dictionary for learners published by Culture Vannin <sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celtic-language_media" title="List of Celtic-language media">List of Celtic-language media</a></div>\n<p>Two weekly programmes in Manx are available on medium wave on Manx Radio: <i>Traa dy liooar</i> on Monday and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140712054609/http://www.manxradio.com/AudioVault/JamysJeheiney.mp3"><i>Jamys Jeheiney</i></a> on Friday. The <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20101128055331/http://manxradio.com/news.aspx?m=70&amp;b1=20&amp;b2=22&amp;b3=24">news in Manx</a> is available on-line from Manx Radio, who have three other weekly programmes that use the language: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140920121748/http://www.manxradio.com/blog.aspx?blogid=14912"><i>Clare ny Gael</i></a>; <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140920121602/http://www.manxradio.com/blog.aspx?blogid=14696"><i>Shiaght Laa</i></a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140904133625/http://www.manxradio.com/blog.aspx?blogid=14872"><i>Moghrey Jedoonee</i></a>.\n</p><p>The <i>Isle of Man Examiner</i> has a monthly bilingual column in Manx.\n</p><p>The first film to be made in Manx \xe2\x80\x93 the 22-minute-long <i>Ny Kirree fo Niaghtey</i> (The Sheep [plural] Under the Snow) \xe2\x80\x93 premiered in 1983 and was entered for the 5th Celtic Film and Television Festival in Cardiff in 1984. It was directed by Shorys Y Creayrie (George Broderick) for Foillan Films of Laxey, and is about the background to an early 18th-century folk song. In 2013, a short film, <a href="/wiki/Solace_in_Wicca" title="Solace in Wicca">Solace in Wicca</a>, was produced with financial assistance from <a href="/wiki/Culture_Vannin" title="Culture Vannin">Culture Vannin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinewood_Group" title="Pinewood Group">CinemaNX</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man_Film" title="Isle of Man Film">Isle of Man Film</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> A series of short cartoons about the life of Cuchulain which were produced by BBC Northern Ireland are available<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> as are a series of cartoons on Manx mythology.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Most significant is a 13-part DVD series Manx translation of the award-winning series <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s9n5kihxGf8&amp;list=PLY5y-gRhKs8jDsAbo2Didfz_f51br-kUx">Friends and Heroes</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signage">Signage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Signage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Manx-museum.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Manx-museum.JPG/200px-Manx-museum.JPG" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Manx-museum.JPG/300px-Manx-museum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Manx-museum.JPG/400px-Manx-museum.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Manx-museum.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Use of Manx on the <a href="/wiki/Manx_Museum" title="Manx Museum">national museum</a>, underneath the English</div></div></div>\n<p>Bilingual road, street, village and town boundary signs are common throughout the Isle of Man. All other road signs are in English only.\n</p><p>\nBusiness signage in Manx is gradually being introduced but is not mandated by law.</p><table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small><i>(November 2010)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Manx_Bible">The Manx Bible</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Manx Bible">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dd><i>In the time of Bishop Wilson it had been a constant source of complaint among the Manx clergy that they were the only church in Christendom that had no version of the Bible in the vulgar tongue. Wilson set to work to remedy the defect, and, with the assistance of some of his clergy, managed to get some of the Bible translated, and the Gospel of St. Matthew printed. Bishop Hildesley, his successor, with the help of the whole body of Manx clergy, completed the work, and in 1775 the whole Bible was printed.</i><sup id="cite_ref-isle-of-man.com_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isle-of-man.com-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>The Bible was first produced in Manx by a group of Anglican clergymen on the island. The Gospel of Matthew was printed in 1748. The four Gospels were produced in 1763 and Conaant Noa nyn Jiarn as Saualtagh Yeesey Creest (the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> of our Lord &amp; Saviour Jesus Christ) in 1767 by the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_the_Propagation_of_Christian_Knowledge" class="mw-redirect" title="Society for the Propagation of Christian Knowledge">Society for the Propagation of Christian Knowledge</a> (SPCK). In 1772 the Old Testament was translated from Hebrew and printed, together with the Books of <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_of_Solomon" class="mw-redirect" title="Wisdom of Solomon">Wisdom of Solomon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiasticus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiasticus">Ecclesiasticus</a> (Sirach) from the <a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">Apocrypha</a>. Yn Vible Casherick (The Holy Bible) of the Old and New Testaments was published as one book by the SPCK in 1775. The bicentenary was celebrated on the Isle of Man in 1975 and included a set of stamps from the Isle of Man Post Office.\n</p><p>This 1775 edition effectively fixed the modern orthography of Manx Gaelic, which has changed little since. Jenner claims that some <a href="/wiki/Bowdlerisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Bowdlerisation">bowdlerisation</a> had occurred in the translation, e.g. the occupation of <a href="/wiki/Rahab" title="Rahab">Rahab</a> the prostitute is rendered as <i>ben-oast</i>,<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_&#123;&#123;&#123;1&#125;&#125;&#125;"><a class="external autonumber" href="//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manx_language#endnote_">[3]</a></sup> a hostess or female inn-keeper.<sup id="cite_ref-isle-of-man.com_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isle-of-man.com-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There was a translation of the Psalmyn Ghavid (Psalms of David) in metre in Manx by the Rev John Clague, vicar of Rushen, which was printed with the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> of 1768. Bishop Hildesley required that these <a href="/wiki/Metrical_Psalms" class="mw-redirect" title="Metrical Psalms">Metrical Psalms</a> were to be sung in churches. These were reprinted by the <a href="/wiki/Manx_Language_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Manx Language Society">Manx Language Society</a> in 1905.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/British_and_Foreign_Bible_Society" title="British and Foreign Bible Society">British and Foreign Bible Society</a> (BFBS) published the Conaant Noa (New Testament) in 1810 and reprinted it in 1824. Yn Vible Casherick (the Holy Bible) of the Old Testament and New Testament (without the two books of the Apocrypha) was first printed as a whole in 1819. BFBS last printed anything on paper in Manx in 1936 when it reprinted Noo Ean (the Gospel of St John); this was reprinted by <a href="/wiki/Manx_Gaelic_Society" title="Manx Gaelic Society">Yn \xc3\x87heshaght Ghailckagh</a> (The Manx Gaelic Society) in 1968. The Manx Bible was republished by Shearwater Press in July 1979 as Bible Chasherick yn Lught Thie (Manx Family Bible), which was a reproduction of the BFBS 1819 Bible.\n</p><p>Since 2014 the BFBS 1936 Manx Gospel of John has been available online on YouVersion and Bibles.org.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church">Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Manx was used in some churches into the late 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-isle-of-man.com_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isle-of-man.com-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Although church services in Manx were once fairly common, they occur infrequently now. Yn Cheshaght Ghailckagh, the Manx Language Society, hold an annual <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vY9VF_bkZsw">Christmas Service</a> at locations around the island.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_and_dialects">Classification and dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Classification and dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Manx is one of the three descendants of <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a> and early Modern Gaelic), and is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>. It shares a number of developments in phonology, vocabulary and grammar with Irish and Scottish Gaelic (in some cases only with dialects of these) and shows a number of unique changes. There are two attested dialects of Manx, Northern Manx and Southern Manx.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> A third dialect may have existed in-between, around Douglas.\n</p><p>Manx shares with Scottish Gaelic the partial loss of contrastive <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalisation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">labial consonants</a>; thus while in Irish the velarised consonants <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p\xcb\xa0 b\xcb\xa0 f\xcb\xa0 w m\xcb\xa0/</span> contrast <a href="/wiki/Phonemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemic">phonemically</a> with palatalised <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p\xca\xb2 b\xca\xb2 f\xca\xb2 v\xca\xb2 m\xca\xb2/</span>, in Scottish Gaelic and Manx, the phonemic contrast has been lost to some extent.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> A consequence of this <a href="/wiki/Phonemic_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemic merger">phonemic merger</a> is that Middle Irish unstressed word-final <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99\xce\xb2\xca\xb2]</span> (spelled <i>-(a)ibh</i>, <i>-(a)imh</i> in Irish and Gaelic) has merged with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99\xce\xb2]</span> (<i>-(e)abh</i>, <i>-(e)amh</i>) in Manx; both have become <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[u]</span>, spelled <i>-oo</i> or <i>-u(e)</i>. Examples include <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">shassoo</i> ("to stand"; Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">seasamh</i>), <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">credjue</i> ("religion"; Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">creideamh</i>), <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">nealloo</i> ("fainting"; Early Modern Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">(i) n\xc3\xa9alaibh</i>, lit. <i>in clouds</i>), and <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">erriu</i> ("on you (plural)"; Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">oraibh</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Medial and final *bh and *mh have become <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/u/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/w/</span> in general in Manx, thus <i>shiu</i> \'you PL\', Scottish and Irish Gaelic <i>sibh</i> (<i>siph</i> in Northern Irish, <i>sib</i> in South Connacht Irish), -bh in final consonant clusters, e.g. Manx <i>sharroo</i> \'bitter\', Scottish <i>searbh</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xca\x83\xc3\xa6rav/</span>, Northern and Western Irish <i>searbh</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xca\x83\xc3\xa6ru/</span>, Southern Irish <i>searbh</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xca\x83\xc3\xa6r\xc9\x99\xce\xb2/</span>, between vowels, e.g. Manx <i>awin</i> \'river\' , Scottish <i>abhainn</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/avi\xc9\xb2/</span>, Irish <i>abhainn</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aun\xca\xb2/</span>, word-finally in monosyllables, e.g. Manx <i>laaue</i> \'hand\', Scottish <i>l\xc3\xa0mh</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/la:v/</span>, Northern Irish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l\xc3\xa6:w/</span>, Western Irish <i>l\xc3\xa1mh</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l\xc9\x91:w/</span>, Southern Irish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l\xc9\x91:\xce\xb2/</span>, at the end of stressed syllables (see further below), as in <i>sourey</i> \'summer\', Scotland and Ireland <i>samhradh</i>, Scottish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/saur\xc9\x99\xc9\xa3/</span>, Northern Irish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/sauru/</span>, Western and Southern Irish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/saur\xc9\x99/</span>. In all this Manx is virtually identical to Northern Irish. Rare retentions of the older pronunciation of "bh" include <i>Divlyn</i>, <i>Divlin</i>, Middle Irish <i>Duibhlinn</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d\xcc\xaau\xce\xb2\xca\xb2l\xca\xb2in\xca\xb2:/</span>, also written <i>Duibhlinn</i> in Modern Irish and Scots Gaelic.\n</p><p>Moreover, similarly to <a href="/wiki/Munster_Irish" title="Munster Irish">Munster Irish</a>, historical <i>bh</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xce\xb2\xca\xb2]</span> and <i>mh</i> (nasalised <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xce\xb2\xca\xb2]</span>) tend to be lost in the middle or at the end of a word in Manx, either with <a href="/wiki/Compensatory_lengthening" title="Compensatory lengthening">compensatory lengthening</a> or vocalisation as <b>u</b> resulting in <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongisation</a> with the preceding vowel. For example, Manx <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">geurey</i> ("winter") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1\xca\xb2eur\xc9\x99], [\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1\xca\xb2u\xcb\x90r\xc9\x99]</span> and <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">sleityn</i> ("mountains") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88sle\xcb\x90d\xca\x92\xc9\x99n]</span> correspond to Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">geimhreadh</i> and <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">sl\xc3\xa9ibhte</i> (Southern Irish dialect spelling and pronunciation <i>g\xc3\xadre</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\x9fi\xcb\x90\xc9\xbe\xca\xb2\xc9\x99]</span>) and <i>sl\xc3\xa9te</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xca\x83l\xca\xb2e\xcb\x90t\xca\xb2\xc9\x99]</span>)).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Another similarity to Munster Irish is the development of the Old Irish diphthongs <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[oi ai]</span> before <a href="/wiki/Velarised" class="mw-redirect" title="Velarised">velarised</a> consonants (spelled <i>ao</i> in Irish and Scottish Gaelic) to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e\xcb\x90]</span> in many words, as in <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">seyr</i> ("carpenter") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[se\xcb\x90r]</span> and <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">keyl</i> ("narrow") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ke\xcb\x90l]</span> (spelled <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">saor</i> and <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">caol</i> in Irish and Scottish, and pronounced virtually the same in Munster).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Like western and northern dialects of Irish (cf. <a href="/wiki/Irish_phonology#Word-initial_consonant_clusters" title="Irish phonology">Irish phonology</a>) and most dialects of Scottish Gaelic, Manx has changed the historical <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/kn \xc9\xa1n mn tn/</span> to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/kr \xc9\xa1r mr tr/</span>. For example, Middle Irish <i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">cn\xc3\xa1id</i> ("mockery") and <i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">mn\xc3\xa1</i> ("women") have become <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">craid</i> and <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">mraane</i> respectively in Manx.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Affrication" class="mw-redirect" title="Affrication">affrication</a> of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t\xcc\xaa\xca\xb2 d\xcc\xaa\xca\xb2]</span> to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t\xca\x83 d\xca\x92]</span> is also common to Manx, northern Irish, and Scottish Gaelic.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Also like northern and western dialects of Irish, as well as like southern dialects of Scottish Gaelic (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Arran" title="Isle of Arran">Arran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kintyre" title="Kintyre">Kintyre</a>), the unstressed word-final syllable <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i\xca\x9d]</span> of Middle Irish (spelled <i>-(a)idh</i> and <i>-(a)igh</i>) has developed to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i\xcb\x90]</span> in Manx, where it is spelled <i>-ee</i>, as in <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">kionnee</i> ("buy"; cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ceannaigh</i>) and <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">cullee</i> ("apparatus"; cf. Gaelic <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">culaidh</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another property Manx shares with Ulster Irish and some dialects of Scottish Gaelic is that <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a/</span> rather than <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99/</span> appears in unstressed syllables before <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/x/</span> (in Manx spelling, <i>agh</i>), for example <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">jeeragh</i> ("straight") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88d\xca\x92i\xcb\x90rax]</span> (Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">d\xc3\xadreach</i>), <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">cooinaghtyn</i> ("to remember") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88ku\xcb\x90n\xca\xb2axt\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99n]</span> (Gaelic <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">cuimhneachd</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Like southern and western varieties of Irish and northern varieties of Scottish Gaelic, but unlike the geographically closer varieties of <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Irish" title="Ulster Irish">Ulster Irish</a> and Arran and Kintyre Gaelic, Manx shows vowel lengthening or diphthongisation before the Old Irish <a href="/wiki/Irish_phonology#Fortis_and_lenis_sonorants" title="Irish phonology">fortis and lenis sonorants</a>. For example, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">cloan</i> ("children") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kl\xc9\x94\xcb\x90n]</span>, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">dhone</i> ("brown") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d\xcc\xaao\xcb\x90n]</span>, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">eeym</i> ("butter") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i\xcb\x90\xe1\xb5\x87m]</span> correspond to Irish/Scottish Gaelic <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">clann</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">donn</i>, and <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">im</i> respectively, which have long vowels or diphthongs in western and southern Irish and in the Scottish Gaelic dialects of the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a>, thus western Irish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kl\xcb\xa0\xc9\x91\xcb\x90n\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0]</span>, Southern Irish/Northern Scottish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kl\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0aun\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0aun\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0]/[d\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0oun\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i\xcb\x90m]/[\xc9\xa4im]</span>), but short vowels and \'long\' consonants in northern Irish, Arran, and Kintyre, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kl\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0an\xcc\xaa\xcb\x90\xcb\xa0]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0on\xcc\xaa\xcb\x90\xcb\xa0]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[im\xca\xb2\xcb\x90]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another similarity with southern Irish is the treatment of Middle Irish word-final unstressed <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99\xc3\xb0]</span>, spelled <i>-(e)adh</i> in Irish and Scottish Gaelic. In nouns (including <a href="/wiki/Verbal_noun" title="Verbal noun">verbal nouns</a>), this became <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99]</span> in Manx, as it did in southern Irish, e.g. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">caggey</i> ("war") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88ka\xcb\x90\xc9\xa3\xc9\x99]</span>, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">moylley</i> ("to praise") <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88m\xc9\x94l\xc9\x99]</span>; cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">cogadh</i> and <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">moladh</i>, pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88k\xcb\xa0\xc9\x94\xc9\xa1\xcb\xa0\xc9\x99]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88m\xcb\xa0\xc9\x94l\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0\xc9\x99]</span> in southern Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Finite_verb" title="Finite verb">finite verb</a> forms before full nouns (as opposed to pronouns) <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99\xc3\xb0]</span> became <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ax]</span> in Manx, as in southern Irish, e.g. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">voyllagh</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88v\xc9\x94lax]</span> ("would praise"), cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">mholfadh</i>, pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xce\xb2\xcb\xa0\xc9\x94l\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0h\xc9\x99x]</span> in southern Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Manx_dialects.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Manx_dialects.png/220px-Manx_dialects.png" width="220" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Manx_dialects.png 1.5x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="355" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Manx_dialects.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dialect map of Manx (boundaries are approximate)</div></div></div>\n<p>Linguistic analysis of the last few dozen native speakers reveals a number of dialectal differences between the northern and the southern parts of the island. Northern Manx is reflected by speakers from towns and villages from <a href="/wiki/Maughold" title="Maughold">Maughold</a> in the northeast of the island to <a href="/wiki/Peel,_Isle_of_Man" title="Peel, Isle of Man">Peel</a> on the west coast. Southern Manx is used by speakers from the <a href="/wiki/Sheading" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheading">Sheading</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rushen" title="Rushen">Rushen</a>. It is possible that written Manx represents a \'midlands\' dialect of Douglas and surrounding areas.\n</p><p>In Southern Manx, older <i>\xc3\xa1</i> and in some cases <i>\xc3\xb3</i> have become <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc3\xa6\xcb\x90]</span>. In Northern Manx the same happens, but <i>\xc3\xa1</i> sometimes remains <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[a\xcb\x90]</span> as well. For example, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">laa</i> ("day", cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">l\xc3\xa1</i>) is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l\xc3\xa6\xcb\x90]</span> in the south but <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l\xc3\xa6\xcb\x90]</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[la\xcb\x90]</span> in the north. Old <i>\xc3\xb3</i> is always <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc3\xa6\xcb\x90]</span> in both dialects, e.g. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">aeg</i> ("young", cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">\xc3\xb3g</i>) is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e\xcb\x90\xc9\xa1]</span> in both dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In many words before <i>rt</i>, <i>rd</i> and <i>rg</i>, and in one or two other words <i>\xc3\xa1</i>, lengthened <i>a</i> and <i>\xc3\xb3</i> have become /\xc5\x93:/, as in <i>paayrt</i> \'part\' /p\xc5\x93:rt/, <i>ard</i> \'high\' /\xc5\x93:rd/, <i>jiarg</i> \'red\' /d\xca\x92\xc5\x93:rg/, <i>argid</i> \'money, silver\' /\xc5\x93:rgid/ and <i>aarey</i> \'gold GEN\' /\xc5\x93:r\xc9\x99/.\n</p><p>In Northern Manx, older <i>(e)a</i> before <i>nn</i> in the same syllable is diphthongised, while in Southern Manx it is lengthened but remains a <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthong</a>. For example, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">kione</i> ("head", cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ceann</i>) is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k\xca\xb2aun]</span> in the north but <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k\xca\xb2o\xcb\x90n]</span> in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Words with <i>ua</i> and in some cases <i>ao</i> in Irish and Scottish are spelled with <i>eay</i> in Manx. In Northern Manx, this sound is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i\xcb\x90]</span>, while in Southern Manx it is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xaf\xcb\x90]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[u\xcb\x90]</span>, or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[y\xcb\x90]</span>. For example, <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">geay</i> ("wind", cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">gaoth</i>) is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1i\xcb\x90]</span> in the north and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1\xc9\xaf\xcb\x90]</span> in the south, while <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">geayl</i> ("coal", cf. Irish <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">gual</i>) is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1i\xcb\x90l]</span> in the north and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1y\xcb\x90l]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1\xc9\xaf\xcb\x90l]</span>, or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1u\xcb\x90l]</span> in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In both the north and the south, there is a tendency to insert a short <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d]</span> sound before a word-final <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[n]</span> in monosyllabic words, as in <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[sle\xe1\xb5\x88n]</span> for <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">slane</i> ("whole") and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[be\xe1\xb5\x88n]</span> for <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ben</i> ("woman"). This phenomenon is known as <a href="/wiki/Pre-occlusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-occlusion">pre-occlusion</a>. In Southern Manx, however, there is pre-occlusion of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d]</span> before <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l]</span> and of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1]</span> before <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc5\x8b]</span>, as in <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xca\x83u\xcb\x90\xe1\xb5\x88l]</span> for <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">shooyl</i> ("walking") and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l\xc9\x94\xe1\xb6\xa2\xc5\x8b]</span> for <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">lhong</i> ("ship"). These forms are generally pronounced without pre-occlusion in the north. Preocclusion of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[b]</span> before <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[m]</span>, on the other hand, is more common in the north, as in <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">trome</i> ("heavy"), which is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t\xcc\xaaro\xe1\xb5\x87m]</span> in the north but <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t\xcc\xaaro\xcb\x90m]</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t\xcc\xaaro\xcb\x90\xe1\xb5\x87m]</span> in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> This feature is also found in <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>.\n</p><p>Southern Manx tends to lose word-initial <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1]</span> before <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l\xca\xb2]</span>, while Northern Manx usually preserves it, e.g. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">glion</i> ("glen") is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1l\xca\xb2\xc9\x94\xe1\xb5\x88n]</span> in the north and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l\xca\xb2\xc9\x94\xe1\xb5\x88n]</span> in the south, and <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">glioon</i> ("knee") is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1l\xca\xb2u\xcb\x90n]</span> in the north and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l\xca\xb2u\xcb\x90\xe1\xb5\x88n]</span> in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phrases">Phrases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Phrases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Some simple conversational words and phrases:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>English (Baarle)</th>\n<th>Manx (Gaelg)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Good morning</td>\n<td>Moghrey mie\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Good afternoon/evening</td>\n<td>Fastyr mie\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Good night</td>\n<td>Oie vie\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>How are you?</td>\n<td>Kys t\'ou? ("tu" form)<br />Kys to shu (plural)<br />Kynas ta shu? ("vous" form)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Very well</td>\n<td>Feer vie\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Thank you</td>\n<td>Gura mie ayd ("tu" form)<br />Gura mie eu ("vous" form)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>And yourself?</td>\n<td>As oo hene?<br />As shiu hene?\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Goodbye</td>\n<td>Slane lhiat<br />Slane lhiu\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Yessir (Manx English equivalent of "man", as a term of address; found as <i>a dhuine</i> in Irish and Scottish)</td>\n<td>Whooiney\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Isle of Man</td>\n<td>Ellan Vannin\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Manx <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> is unlike that of Irish and Scottish Gaelic, both of which use closely related modernised variants of the orthography of <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Irish" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern Irish">Early Modern Irish</a>, the language of the educated Gaelic elite of both Ireland and Scotland (where it is called <a href="/wiki/Classical_Gaelic" title="Classical Gaelic">Classical Gaelic</a>) until the mid-19th century. These orthographies in general show both word pronunciation and word derivation from the Gaelic past, though not in a one-to-one system, there being only 18 letters to represent around 50 phonemes. While Manx in effect uses the English alphabet, except for &#x27e8;x&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;z&#x27e9;, the 24 letters of its alphabet likewise do not cover a similar range of phonemes, and therefore many digraphs and trigraphs are used.\n</p><p>The orthography was developed by people who were unaware of traditional Gaelic orthography, as they had learned literacy in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> and English (the initial development in the 16th century), then only English (later developments). Therefore, the orthography shows the pronunciation of words mainly from the point of view of early Modern English "phonetics", and to a small extent Welsh, rather than from the Gaelic point of view.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The result is an inconsistent and only partially phonetic spelling system, in a similar way as spelling in English is inconsistent and only partially phonetic. <a href="/wiki/T._F._O%27Rahilly" title="T. F. O&#39;Rahilly">T.&#160;F. O\'Rahilly</a> expressed the opinion that Gaelic in the Isle of Man was saddled with a corrupt spelling which is neither traditional nor phonetic; if the traditional Gaelic orthography had been preserved, the close kinship that exists between Manx Gaelic and Scottish Gaelic would be obvious to all at first sight.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is no evidence of <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_type" title="Gaelic type">Gaelic script</a> having been used on the island.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cedilla">Cedilla</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cedilla">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Manx uses relatively few <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritics</a>, but a <a href="/wiki/Cedilla" title="Cedilla">cedilla</a> is often (but not exclusively) used to differentiate between the two pronunciations of <i>ch</i>:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>\xc3\x87hiarn</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xcb\x88t\xcd\xa1\xca\x83a\xcb\x90rn/</span>), meaning <i>lord</i>, is pronounced with the <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar affricate">palato-alveolar affricate</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t\xcd\xa1\xca\x83/</span>, as in the English "<b>ch</b>urch"</li>\n<li><i>Chamoo</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/xa\xcb\x88mu</span>), meaning <i>nor</i> or <i>neither</i>, is pronounced with the <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">velar fricative</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/x/</span>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Scottish_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Scottish English">Scottish</a> pronunciation of the word "lo<b>ch</b>" (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xcb\x88l\xc9\x92x/</span>), a sound which is commonly represented by <i>gh</i> at the ends of words in Manx (as it often is in the English of Ireland).</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The following examples are taken from Broderick 1984\xe2\x80\x9386, 1:178\xe2\x80\x9379 and 1:350\xe2\x80\x9353. The first example is from a speaker of Northern Manx, the second from <a href="/wiki/Ned_Maddrell" title="Ned Maddrell">Ned Maddrell</a>, a speaker of Southern Manx.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Orthography\n</th>\n<th>Phonetic transcription\n</th>\n<th>Gloss\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">V\'ad smooinaghtyn dy beagh cabbyl jeeaghyn skee as deinagh ayns y voghree dy beagh eh er ve ec ny ferrishyn fud ny h-oie as beagh ad cur lesh yn saggyrt dy cur e vannaght er.</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">vod\xcc\xaa \xcb\x88smu\xcb\x90n\xca\xb2axt\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99n d\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99 bi\xc9\x99x \xcb\x88ka\xcb\x90b\xc9\x99l d\xca\x92i\xcb\x90\xc9\x99n ski\xcb\x90 as \xcb\x88d\xcc\xaa\xc3\xb8in\xca\xb2ax uns \xc9\x99 \xcb\x88vo\xcb\x90x\xc9\x99ri d\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99 bi\xc9\x99x e er vi ek n\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88feri\xca\x83\xc9\x99n fod\xcc\xaa n\xc9\x99 h\xc3\xb8i as bi\xc9\x99x \xc9\x99d\xcc\xaa k\xc3\xb8r le\xca\x83 \xc9\x99n \xcb\x88sa\xcb\x90\xc9\xa1\xc9\x99rt\xcc\xaa d\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99 k\xc3\xb8r \xc9\x99 \xcb\x88vanax er</span>\n</td>\n<td>They used to think if a horse was looking tired and weary in the morning then it had been with the fairies all night and they would bring the priest to put his blessing on it.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Va ben aynshoh yn \xc3\xa7hiaghtin chaie as v\'ee laccal mish dy ynsagh ee dy gra yn Padjer yn \xc3\x87hiarn. Dooyrt ee dy row ee gra eh tra v\'ee inneen veg, agh t\'eh ooilley jarroodit eck, as v\'ee laccal gynsagh eh reesht son dy gra eh ec vrastyl ny red ennagh. As dooyrt mish dy jinnagh mee jannoo my share son dy cooney lhee as ren ee \xc3\xa7heet aynshoh son dy clashtyn eh, as vel oo laccal dy clashtyn mee dy gra eh?</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">v\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88b\xc9\x9bn \xc9\x99\xcb\x88so\xcb\x90 \xc9\x99n \xcb\x88t\xca\x83a\xcb\x90n \xcb\x88kai as vai \xcb\x88la\xcb\x90l \xcb\x88mi\xca\x83 \xc3\xb0i \xcb\x88jin\xc3\xb0ax i \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88\xc9\xa1re\xcb\x90 in \xcb\x88pa\xcb\x90d\xca\x92\xc9\x99r \xc9\x99n \xcb\x88t\xca\x83a\xcb\x90rn \xe2\x80\x96 d\xcc\xaaot\xcc\xaa i \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88rau i \xc9\xa1re\xcb\x90 a \xcb\x88t\xcc\xaare\xcb\x90 vai i\xcb\x88n\xca\xb2in \xcb\x88ve\xcb\x90\xc9\xa1 \xe2\x80\x96 ax t\xcc\xaae \xcb\x88ol\xca\xb2u d\xca\x92a\xcb\x88rud\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99t\xca\x83 ek \xe2\x80\x96 as vei \xcb\x88la\xcb\x90l \xcb\x88\xc9\xa1\xca\xb2in\xc3\xb0ax a \xcb\x88ri\xcb\x90\xca\x83 san \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88\xc9\xa1re\xcb\x90 \xc9\x99 \xc9\x99\xc9\xa1 \xcb\x88vra\xcb\x90st\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99l n\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88ri\xc3\xb0 \xc9\x99nax \xe2\x80\x96 as \xcb\x88d\xcc\xaaut\xcc\xaa mi\xca\x83 \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88d\xca\x92inax mi \xcb\x88d\xca\x92inu m\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88\xca\x83e\xcb\x90 san \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88kun\xc9\x99 l\xca\xb2ei as \xcb\x88ren\xca\xb2 i \xcb\x88t\xca\x83it\xcc\xaa o\xcb\x88so\xcb\x90 san \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88kla\xcb\x90\xca\x83t\xca\xb2\xc9\x99n a \xe2\x80\x96 as vel u \xcb\x88la\xcb\x90l \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88kla\xcb\x90\xca\x83t\xca\xb2\xc9\x99n mi \xc3\xb0\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88\xc9\xa1re\xcb\x90 a \xe2\x80\x96</span>\n</td>\n<td>There was a woman here last week and she wanted me to teach her to say the Lord\'s Prayer. She said that she used to say it when she was a little girl, but she has forgotten it all, and she wanted to learn it again to say it at a class or something. And I said I would do my best to help her and she came here to hear it, and do you want to hear me say it?\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span id="Gaelic_versions_of_the_Lord.27s_Prayer"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaelic_versions_of_the_Lord\'s_Prayer">Gaelic versions of the Lord\'s Prayer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gaelic versions of the Lord&#039;s Prayer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord&#39;s Prayer">Lord\'s Prayer</a> has been translated into all the Goidelic tongues. Although the wordings are not completely cognate, they demonstrate the different orthographies.\n</p>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<dl><dd><b>The standard version of the Lord\'s Prayer in Manx</b></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>Ayr ain t\'ayns niau,</dd>\n<dd>Casherick dy row dt\'ennym.</dd>\n<dd>Dy jig dty reeriaght.</dd>\n<dd>Dt\'aigney dy row jeant er y thalloo,</dd>\n<dd>myr t\'ayns miolagh,</dd>\n<dd>agh livrey shin veih olk:</dd>\n<dd>Son lhiats y reeriaght, as y phooar, as y ghloyr, son dy bragh as dy bragh.</dd>\n<dd>Amen.</dd></dl>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<dl><dd><b>Manx version of 1713</b> (with long S)<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Ayr Ain, t\'ayns Niau;</dd>\n<dd>Ca\xc5\xbfherick dy rou dt\'ennym;</dd>\n<dd>Di jig dty Reereeaght;</dd>\n<dd>Dt\'aigney dy rou jeant er y Talloo myr ta ayns Niau;</dd>\n<dd>Cur dooin nyn Arran jiu as gagh veih olk;</dd>\n<dd>Son liats y Reereeaght y Phooar as y Ghloyr, \xc5\xbfon dy bragh as dy bragh.</dd>\n<dd>Amen</dd></dl>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table thainm.</dd>\n<dd>Tost do flaithius.</dd>\n<dd>Did do toil i talmain</dd>\n<dd>amail ata in nim.</dd>\n<dd>Tabair dun indiu ar sasad lathi.</dd>\n<dd>Ocus log dun ar fiachu</dd>\n<dd>amail logmaitne diar fhechemnaib.</dd>\n<dd>Ocus nis lecea sind i n-amus n-dofulachtai.</dd>\n<dd>Acht ron soer o cech ulc.</dd>\n<dd>Amen ropfir.</dd></dl>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 33.33%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<dl><dd><b>The Prayer in modern Irish</b></dd>\n<dd>\xc3\x81r n-Athair, at\xc3\xa1 ar neamh:</dd>\n<dd>go naofar d\'ainm (alt. go naomha\xc3\xadthear
