Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k\xca\xb2iad]</span>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">ceud</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Help:IPA/Scottish Gaelic">[k\xca\xb2\xca\xb0i\xc9\x99t\xcc\xaa]</a></span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Syntax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Like most Insular Celtic languages, Manx uses <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object" title="Verb\xe2\x80\x93subject\xe2\x80\x93object">verb\xe2\x80\x93subject\xe2\x80\x93object</a> word order: the inflected verb of a sentence precedes the <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a>, which itself precedes the <a href="/wiki/Direct_object" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct object">direct object</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> However, as noted above, most finite verbs are formed periphrastically, using an auxiliary verb in conjunction with the verbal noun. In this case, only the auxiliary verb precedes the subject, while the verbal noun comes after the subject. The auxiliary verb may be a <a href="/wiki/Modal_verb" title="Modal verb">modal verb</a> rather than a form of <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">bee</i> ("be") or <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">jannoo</i> ("do"). Particles like the negative <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">cha</i> ("not") precede the inflected verb. Examples:\n</p>\n<div style="margin-left:2em">\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">main<br />verb</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2"><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">subject</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2"><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">direct<br />object</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Hug</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">yn</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">saggyrt</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">e</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">laue</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">urree.</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>put-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">PRET</span></span></td>\n<td>the</td>\n<td>priest</td>\n<td>his</td>\n<td>hand</td>\n<td>on her\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="6">"The priest put his hand on her."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>&#160;\n</p>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">aux.<br />verb</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2"><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">subject</span>\n</td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">main<br />verb</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2"><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">direct<br />object</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Va</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ny</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">eayin</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">gee</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">yn</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">conney.</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>were</td>\n<td>the</td>\n<td>lambs</td>\n<td>eat-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">V.N.</span></span></td>\n<td>the</td>\n<td>gorse\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="6">"The lambs used to eat the gorse."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>&#160;\n</p>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td></td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">modal<br />verb</span></td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">subject</span></td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">main<br />verb</span></td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">direct<br />object</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Cha</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">jarg</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">shiu</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">fakin</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">red erbee.</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>not</td>\n<td>can</td>\n<td>you-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">PL</span></span></td>\n<td>see-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">V.N.</span></span></td>\n<td>anything\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="5">"You can\'t see anything."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<p>When the auxiliary verb is a form of <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">jannoo</i> ("do"), the direct object precedes the verbal noun and is connected to it with the particle <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">y</i>:\n</p>\n<div style="margin-left:2em">\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">aux.<br />verb</span></td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">subject</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2"><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">direct<br />object</span>\n</td>\n<td></td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">main<br />verb</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Ren</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ad</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">my</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">choraa</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">y</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">chlashtyn.</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>did</td>\n<td>they</td>\n<td>my</td>\n<td>voice</td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">PARTICLE</span></span></td>\n<td>hear-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">V.N.</span></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="6">"They heard my voice."<sup id="cite_ref-Thoms105_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thoms105-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<p>As in Irish (cf. <a href="/wiki/Irish_syntax#The_forms_meaning_&quot;to_be&quot;" title="Irish syntax">Irish syntax#The forms meaning "to be"</a>), there are two ways of expressing "to be" in Manx: with the substantive verb <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">bee</i>, and with the copula. The substantive verb is used when the <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(grammar)" title="Predicate (grammar)">predicate</a> is an adjective, adverb, or <a href="/wiki/Prepositional_phrase" class="mw-redirect" title="Prepositional phrase">prepositional phrase</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Examples:\n</p>\n<div style="margin-left:2em">\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">t\'</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">eh</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">agglagh</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>is</td>\n<td>it</td>\n<td>awful/frightening\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="3">"It is awful/frightening."\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>&#160;\n</p>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">t\'</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">eh</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">dy mie</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>is</td>\n<td>he</td>\n<td>well\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="3">"He is well"\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>&#160;\n</p>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">t\'</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">eh</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ayns</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">y</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">thie-oast</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>is</td>\n<td>he</td>\n<td>in</td>\n<td>the</td>\n<td>house-ale (<a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pub</a>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="5">"He is in the ale-house (<a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pub</a>)."\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<p>Where the predicate is a noun, it must be converted to a prepositional phrase headed by the preposition <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">in</i> ("in") + possessive pronoun (agreeing with the subject) in order for the substantive verb to be grammatical:\n</p>\n<div style="margin-left:2em">\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">t\'</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">eh</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ny</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">wooinney</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">mie</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>is</td>\n<td>he</td>\n<td>in-his</td>\n<td>man</td>\n<td>good\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="5">"He is a good man" (lit. "He is in his good man")<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<p>Otherwise, the copula is used when the predicate is a noun. The copula itself takes the form <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">is</i> or <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">she</i> in the present tense, but it is often omitted in affirmative statements:\n</p>\n<div style="margin-left:2em">\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">She</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Manninagh</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">mish</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">COPULA</span></span></td>\n<td>Manxman</td>\n<td>me\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="3">"I am a Manxman."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>&#160;\n</p>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Shoh</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">\'n</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">dooinney</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>this</td>\n<td>the</td>\n<td>man\n</td>\n<td colspan="3">"This is the man."<sup id="cite_ref-Thoms105_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thoms105-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<p>In questions and negative sentences, the present tense of the copula is <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">nee</i>:\n</p>\n<div style="margin-left:2em">\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Cha</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">nee</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">mish</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">eh</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>not</td>\n<td><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">COPULA</span></span></td>\n<td>me</td>\n<td>him\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="4">"I am not him."<sup id="cite_ref-Thoms105_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thoms105-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>&#160;\n</p>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">Nee</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">shoh</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">\'n</i></td>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">lioar?</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">COPULA</span></span></td>\n<td>this</td>\n<td>the</td>\n<td>book\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="4">"Is this the book?"<sup id="cite_ref-Thoms105_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thoms105-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Manx vocabulary is predominantly of Goidelic origin, derived from Old Irish and closely related to words in Irish and Scottish Gaelic. However, Manx itself, as well as the languages from which it is derived, borrowed words from other languages as well, especially <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman</a>), and English (both <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> and Modern English).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The following table shows a selection of nouns from the <a href="/wiki/Swadesh_list" title="Swadesh list">Swadesh list</a> and indicates their pronunciations and etymologies.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Manx\n</th>\n<th>IPA<sup id="cite_ref-Brodvol2_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brodvol2-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th>English\n</th>\n<th>Etymology<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">aane</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e\xcb\x90n]</span>\n</td>\n<td>liver\n</td>\n<td>Goidelic; from Mid.Ir. <i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">ae</i> &lt; O.Ir. <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">\xc3\xb3a</i>; cf. Ir. <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ae</i>, Sc.G. <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">adha</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">aer</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e\xcb\x90\xc9\x99]</span>\n</td>\n<td>sky\n</td>\n<td>Latin; from O.Ir. <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">aer</i> &lt; L. <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">a\xc3\xabr</i>; cf. Sc.G. <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">adhar</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">aile</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ail]</span>\n</td>\n<td>fire\n</td>\n<td>Goidelic; from O.Ir. <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">aingel</i> "very bright"; cf. Ir., Sc.G. <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">aingeal</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ardnieu</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99rd\xcc\xaa\xcb\x88n\xca\xb2eu]</span>\n</td>\n<td>snake\n</td>\n<td>Apparently "highly poisonous" (cf. <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ard</i> "high", <i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">nieu</i> "poison")\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">awin</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[aun\xca\xb2], [\xcb\x88aw\xc9\x99n\xca\xb2]</span>\n</td>\n<td>river\n</td>\n<td>Goidelic; from the M.Ir. dative form <i>abainn</i> of <i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">aba</i> &lt; O.Ir. <i>abaind</i> <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">aba</i>; cf. Ir. <i>abha</i>/<i>abhainn</i>, dative <i>abhainn</i>, Sc.G. <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">abhainn</i> (literary nominative <i>abha</i>).\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gv" title="Manx language text">ayr</i>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88e\xcb\x90ar]</span>\n</td>\n<td>father\n</td>\n<td>Goidelic; from M.Ir. <i>athair</i>, O.Ir. <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">athir</i>; cf. The name means "mousy grey" in Manx.</div></div></div>\n<p>Foreign loanwords are primarily <a href="/wiki/Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse language">Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, with a smaller number coming from French. Some examples of Norse loanwords are <i>garey</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Garden" title="Garden">garden</a>", from <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gar%C3%B0r" class="extiw" title="wikt:gar\xc3\xb0r">gar\xc3\xb0r</a></i>, "enclosure") and <i>sker</i> meaning a sea rock (from <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sker" class="extiw" title="wikt:sker">sker</a></i>, compare with <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/skj%C3%A6r#Etymology_2" class="extiw" title="wikt:skj\xc3\xa6r">skj\xc3\xa6r</a></i> and <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sker#Icelandic" class="extiw" title="wikt:sker">sker</a></i>). Examples of French loanwords are <i>danjeyr</i> ("danger", from <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/danger#French" class="extiw" title="wikt:danger">danger</a></i>) and <i>vondeish</i> ("advantage", from <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/avantage" class="extiw" title="wikt:avantage">avantage</a></i>).\n</p><p>English loanwords were common in late (pre-revival) Manx, e.g. <i>boy</i> ("boy"), <i>badjer</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Badger" title="Badger">badger</a>"), rather than the more usual Gaelic <i>guilley</i> and <i>brock</i>. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jenner" title="Henry Jenner">Henry Jenner</a>, on asking someone what he was doing, was told <i>Ta mee smokal pipe</i> ("I am smoking a pipe"), and that "[he] certainly considered that he was talking Manx, and not English, in saying it." In more recent years, there has been a reaction against such borrowing, resulting in coinages for technical vocabulary. Despite this, <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calques</a> exist in Manx, not necessarily obvious to its speakers.\n</p><p>Some religious terms come ultimately from Latin, Greek and Hebrew, e.g. <i>casherick</i> (holy), from the Latin <i>consecr\xc4\x81tus</i>; <i>mooinjer</i> (people) from the Latin <i>monasterium</i> (originally a <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a>; <i>agglish</i> (church) from the Greek <i>\xe1\xbc\x90\xce\xba\xce\xba\xce\xbb\xce\xb7\xcf\x83\xce\xaf\xce\xb1</i> (<i>ekklesia</i>, literally meaning assembly) and <i>abb</i> (<a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbot</a>) from the Hebrew "\xd7\x90\xd7\x91\xd7\x90" (<i>abba</i>, meaning "father"). These did not necessarily come directly into Manx, but via Old Irish. In more recent times, <i>ulpan</i> has been borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew" title="Modern Hebrew">modern Hebrew</a>. Many Irish and English loanwords also have a classical origin, e.g. <i>\xc3\xa7hellveeish</i> (Irish <i>teilef\xc3\xads</i>) and <i>\xc3\xa7hellvane</i> meaning <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telephone" title="Telephone">telephone</a> respectively. Foreign language words (usually known via English) are used occasionally especially for ethnic food, e.g. chorizo, spaghetti.\n</p><p>To fill gaps in recorded Manx vocabulary, revivalists have referred to modern Irish and Scottish Gaelic for words and inspiration.\n</p><p>Going in the other direction, Manx Gaelic has influenced <a href="/wiki/Manx_English" title="Manx English">Manx English</a> (Anglo-Manx). Common words and phrases in Anglo-Manx originating in the language include <i>tholtan</i> (the "th" is pronounced as a "t") meaning a ruined farmhouse, <i>quaaltagh</i> meaning a <a href="/wiki/First-foot" title="First-foot">first-foot</a>, <i>keeill</i> meaning a church (especially an old one), <i><a href="/wiki/Cammag" title="Cammag">cammag</a></i>, <i>traa-dy-liooar</i> meaning "time enough", and <a href="/wiki/Tynwald" title="Tynwald">Tynwald</a> (<i>tinvaal</i>), which is ultimately of Norse origin, but comes via Manx. It is suggested that the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Keys" title="House of Keys">House of Keys</a> takes its name from <i>Kiare as Feed</i> (four and twenty), which is the number of its sitting members.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_vocabulary_examples">Comparative vocabulary examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manx_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Comparative vocabulary examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<center>\n<table cellspacing="7" class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="background:#ffdead;">Manx
